0743437497|5.0|Though at first glance, THE SHINING is simply a story about a haunted house (certainly something that has been done before), it remains one of the best stories I have EVER read.  If you are thinking of picking up this book, the best thing you can do is stop thinking about it and start reading it.  Not only is it one of Stephen King's best, most masterful tales, it is one of the best suspense novels written today.  And not because it is supernaturally horrifying (though it certainly has it's moments).  But rather, because the most chilling part of THE SHINING is how King takes you down a path of madness that feels a little too real for fiction.<br /><br />THE SHINING builds slowly.  We get to know the Torrence family - Jack, a recovering alcoholic who struggles to find steady work but longs to support his family; Wendy, his wife, who loves him despite some past physical abuse at the hands of Jack's temper and his love for gin, and Danny, their young son who holds the struggling family together.  Both parents, however dysfunctional desire more than anything to raise their son and love him better than their parents loved them.  Their imperfection is that more painful because of this simple, noble desire.<br /><br />But Danny is not just any young boy.  He has what King calls \\"The Shining\\".  The ability to hear others thoughts, feel their feelings, and occasionally get a glimpse of the future.  And when Jack takes a job at the Overlook Hotel as its winter caretaker, Danny's premonitions grow darker and darker.<br /><br />Imagine, a huge, old historic hotel up in the bitterly cold and isolating Colorado Mountains.  It's tucked away for the winter.  The rooms are locked, there are sheets over much of the furniture.  The chairs are up in the grand ballroom. It is just you and your family for several months - cooking in the great kitchen, enjoying several of the rooms to yourselves, and wandering the halls when there is nothing else to do.  It creaks and groans as it settles in, and you're its only heart; the only thing alive in all the cold.<br /><br />The haunting begins almost immediately.  From strange bumps in the night, to the moving of topiary animals, to certain presences destined to live out their final moments their old rooms.  But King escalates these occurrences until the hotel begins to come to life with all its old and darkest characters.  And they are looking for new members to join them - to stay forever.  But they won't kill you to do that.  Rather, they'd rather have you do it yourself.<br /><br />Thus begins a story of madness and seduction for Jack.  Despite the fact that he no longer drinks; despite the fact that position at The Overlook hotel represents a new start for his family; despite the fact that he and his wife begin to rediscover why they are together, the house begins to whisper in his ear.  It sends him thoughts of his own father - another physical abuser.  It reminds him of his thirst.  It puts twisted thoughts in his head of how Wendy and even Danny don't respect him, don't trust him and secretly feel that their lives would be better without him.  And finally, it reintroduces him to his great foil.  The ghost of the bartender Lloyd visits him more and more often, inviting him to the grandest parties and the smoothest drinks as the lines between madness and reality, the past and the present blur into one.  He fights, he fights, and then he falls to the hotel and its desire to keep them all there.  As the family and the hotel unravel into their worst versions of themselves, it's hard to tell what is most frightening - the ghosts of the dead or the dark hearts of the living.  Towards the end, Wendy thinks to herself, \\"There was a stale odor of gin and olives about him that seemed to set off an old terror in her, a worse terror than any hotel could provide by itself.\\"<br /><br />In the end (and don't worry, I won't give away the end), this story comes down to a twisted and destructive love of family.  Andwhether or not the darkest vices can truly destroy the bonds between a father and a son.<br /><br />I envy you, new reader!  For you get to read this amazing story for the first time.  Enjoy it.  And when you get to chapters \\"Inside 217\\" and \\"217 Revisited\\" make sure you're alone, and it's quiet...and dark.<br /><br />Carrie Salo<br />Author of the Supernatural Thriller - The Soundingwhether or not the darkest vices can truly destroy the bonds between a father and a son.<br /><br />I envy you, new reader! 0142000671|5.0|**This review may contain spoilers**<br /><br />Oh, boy, it's \\"Hamlet Syndrome work\\" time again.  I define \\"Hamlet Syndrome work\\" as a work of literature that has been analyzed, dissected, and discussed so thoroughly that it's hard to say anything new or original about it. Steinbeck's enduring novella \\"Of Mice and Men\\" (hereafter OM&M) certainly qualifies. OM&M has permeated life and society's consciousness, at least American life and consciousness, probably because it unpretentiously depicts the main existential question of life: Is it worth living? Should we bother living?  At first glance, OM&M, from its title onward, seems to suggest NO.<br /><br />We have the classic split personality \\"brains and brawn\\" duo in George and Lennie.  Despite their respective qualities, they could be considered life's hopeless losers. They are impoverished migrant workers and even fugitives because of an assault the mentally handicapped Lennie inadvertently committed upon a woman.  Despite his vast strength, Lennie is a high-maintenance, perpetual man-baby who needs almost constant supervision.  George profanely complains about the burden Lennie poses.  Like just about all the other characters in OM&M, they feel isolated and desperate, or at least they would if they didn't have each other.  Nevertheless, defying the odds, George manages to wrangle himself and Lennie farm worker jobs.<br /><br />Things seems to settle down well.  George and Lennie have saved money from previous jobs.  The grizzled, dejected old custodian Candy has some money.  Perhaps together they can buy a slice of earthly paradise: a rabbit farm where Lennie can live safe and secure and pet his animal friends.  But the boss's ostracized, belligerent son Curley and his bored, ignored flirtatious wife eventually turn out to be serpents lying in the way.  Lennie, again unintentionally, cripples one and kills the other.  Earthly paradise goes up in smoke, but, some may argue, George mercifully gives Lennie a way to an otherworldly paradise.<br /><br />OM&M portrays the apparently dead-end, humdrum, lonesome, hopeless rural world of Depression-era America.  But like some great works of literature, it finds hope where there is no hope to be found.  Despite their hard-luck lives, George and Lennie together function as an effective unit.  They nurture a dream and keep going because of it.  Candy helps bring their dream tantalizingly close. Slim, the manager, sympathizes with the duo and does what he can to protect them.  George's mercy killing of Lennie after his manslaughter may be the kindest thing he ever did for Lennie.  He keeps the dream alive for Lennie even as he shoots him in the back of the head.  Slim arranges to have George's homicide appear to be self-defense and even be a shoulder to lean on as George glumly contemplates his \\"freedom\\" from Lennie.<br /><br />OM&M seems to show life as an inevitable tragedy.  However, it shows subtle suggestions that it need not have happened.  Mainly, if George had supervised Lenny a bit more carefully, or had other characters like Crooks the shunned but dignified African-American stablehand (with whom Lenny had struck up a grudging friendship) watch out for him a little more, things may have turned out differently.  OM&M, I think, implies that life could be better and worth living if we WERE a little nicer to each other, cared a little more for each other, helped each other a little more.  This solicitude, coupled with our capacity for hope in even the bleakest situations, is the key to bliss.  Therefore, if God sees our hopes, our plans, our resolve, maybe He really isn't laughing. Maybe He is actually rooting for us.otherworldly paradise.<br /><br />OM&M portrays the apparently dead-end, humdrum, lonesome, hopeless rural world of Depression-era America. 0307593312|4.0|My Tokyo-Boy husband read this in Japanese while I read it in English. (He's not done yet.) And so cultural difference was at the forefront of my brain when reading 1Q84.<br /><br />On the surface, this book is about two young adults who knew eachother once and are looking for a way to reconnect. Both of them have cut ties with their fanatical families and through a cult and a young author of a fantasy novel they reconnect.<br /><br />The Japanese version of 1Q84 is actually three, separate novels. This makes total sense when reading the English version as it does seem to be three different stories: Tengo and Aomame as they enter 1Q84 with Aomame climbing down the expressway stairs and Tengo getting caught up in the writing of Air Chrysalis, Aomame's deadly errand related to the cult, and then finally Ushikawa's surveillance as Tengo hangs with his dad in Chikura (Cat Town).<br /><br />The first part of the novel was extremely interesting. I can't say I actually got \\"drawn into\\" the story, it was more like sinking into a hot bath, immersing myself in the strange, vague-but-detailed world of Tengo and Aomamae and then reveling as each secret connection between them is revealed.<br /><br />For me, the third part went on too long. Way, way, way too long. Murakami is at his recursive best throughout the novel. He constantly has the characters bringing back images and scenes, goes over in detail their backhistory and their moods, and seems to enjoy revisiting each moment again and again like a child picking at a scab.<br /><br />What's revealed beneath these recursive events is the raw, exposed feelings of the characters themselves. That is what I treasure in a Murakami book.<br /><br />But the last third of the book was needlessly drawn out. Although it ends in a pleasantly circular manner (unlike some of his earlier works where too many questions and fates are left hanging), and all the loose ends are tied up, I felt this could have been accomplished without the unnecessary heavy intrusion of Ushizawa.<br /><br />Still, this is Murakami, and part of the journey of one of his books is to follow his unrushed, laid-back, endless-mundane-detailed prose as it creates layer upon layer of images on you so much that you can almost taste the air the characters breathe.<br /><br />And there are moments that strike me as unbelievably odd-but-apt, like this description of Tengo being woken up by the phone:<br /><br />\\"..his voice still slurry from sleep. It was like his head was filled with frozen lettuce. There must be people who don't know you're not supposed to freeze lettuce. Once lettuce has been frozen, it loses all its crispness- which for lettuce is surely its best characteristic.\\"<br /><br />Finally, there's the overwhelming physical-sensual nature of how all the main characters think and act. It is hard not to react to Tengo's obsession with his memory of his mother's sexual act with a stranger and Aomame's pick up of the balding, middle-aged man with the mixed-revulsion-fascination that my American upbringing as inculcated into me.<br /><br />If I somehow step aside from those first reactions, remembering how sex and naked bodies and female desires are treated differently in Japan, then these images and casual attitudes towards sex becomes a physical embodiment of the strange, dream-like emotional connections that occur between the characters.<br /><br />But it takes conscious effort to read the book that way, and I imagine for some Americans their own visceral reactions might be a giant elephant in the room.<br /><br />This is not a book I would recommend to anyone. You have to be willing to give up parts of yourself; the ones wanting concrete answers and real-world logic. The ones that are too biased by American ideas of the plot-shapes of American novels and American sexual mores.<br /><br />However, it is the novel by Murakami, despite the frustrating drawn-out nature of the last third of the book, that expresses best his ability to take the completely unbelievable and fantastical and superimpose it on simple, mundane lives in order to show the beauty of what it means to be human and alone.<br /><br />This Book's Snack Rating: A bag of kaki-no-tani (peanuts mixed with small rice crackers) where the more you eat, the more your tongue catches on fire from the spiced crackers for the layers and layers of characterization and story. You need a sip of whiskey or a cold beer just to keep going.ity to take the completely unbelievable and fantastical and superimpose it on simple, mundane lives in order to show the beauty of what it means to be human and alone.<br /><br />This Book's Snack Rating: A bag of kaki-no-tani (peanuts mixed with small rice crackers) where the more you eat, the more your tongue catches on fire from the spiced crackers for the layers and layers of characterization and story. B0057VDGNK|3.0|(3 1/2 stars) It's hard not to compare the original sound track for \\"Drive\\" to the 1980's soundtrack for [[ASIN:B000000OY3 To Live & Die In L.A.: Original Motion Picture Soundtrack]], because the movie \\"Drive\\" itself was such an homage to that film. If you've seen \\"To Live & Die\\", you'll immediately think of it when you hear \\"Drive\\" for the first time, especially if you are sitting in a theater.<br /><br />And that soundtrack set the bar very high for OSTs, even though it was done by one of the 1980's largely forgotten groups in Wang Chung. If not for a horrible name with an even more regrettable Top-40 radio lyric (\\"everybody have fun tonight, everybody Wang Chung tonight\\"), that band would easily have been known for one of the best soundtracks of all time rather than for high school reunion mix tapes.<br /><br />How does \\"Drive\\" do? Well, it's certainly very good, and very integral to the film. It has a distinct '80s sound but for the most part does not sound cheesy. The feel is instead very moody and atmospheric (think Miami Vice before it jumped the shark, or maybe even a few elements of Scarface), with a mix of a few vocal songs but predominantly ambient instrumentals. If you saw the movie, this will easily transport you back to it.<br /><br />Some tracks are better than others (and some aren't even really songs), so in my opinion you'll either use this as background music if you want to set a certain tone or you will likely rearrange the order of things on your phone and jettison several of them.  As a soundtrack, in relation to it's importance and effectiveness within the movie, this is close to a 5 star effort in that context.  As a stand alone album, especially if you haven't seen the film, much more of a 3 star.<br /><br />Some may say that is not a valid criticism, but compared to an OST like \\"To Live & Die\\", what brought that soundtrack to perfection was that it worked on ALL levels. It was an integral tone setter for the movie. It was a great stand alone album. You didn't have to see the movie to enjoy most of the tracks (which I think is the case here). \\"Drive\\" is more like [[ASIN:B00005BJJX Aliens: The Deluxe Edition]], not in any direct musical sense, but that most of its contents are not stand alone songs and need to be taken as a whole to work well. While much of it is very good, there are a few tracks that do sound more like those elements of the 80' we are trying to forget.<br /><br />With that being said, this soundtrack is an easy pickup for those who have seen the film and liked it. Overall, a solid 3 1/2 star effort you won't be disappointed in as long as you know what you're expecting.e album. B00723NWJE|4.0|For fans wanting more from last year's [[ASIN:B004E0Z4XK Rome]], which was an interesting soundtrack concept album for a fictitious 1970's-era Italian movie (honest!), by Danger Mouse, Daniele Luppi, and Norah Jones, this ain't it. There are a few stylistic teases, and the collaboration partially returns with Danger Mouse's co-writing. But \\"Broken Hearts\\" comes across first and foremost as a Norah Jones album, and you get the sense she has now had her break out debut, played the field while sowing her musical oats, and is settling into a style she wants to define rather than the other way around.<br /><br />This is a solid atmospheric effort that has grown on me. It is not straight up chill by any means, but many of the tracks can put you in a mellow groove as long as you listen to the music more than the lyrics (if \\"Broken Hearts\\" is a soundtrack about anything, it is for the story of a relationship's very bad end). Overall has a very vintage sound, but not an outdated one. I wish I had bought it on a LP as opposed to CD, I sometimes got the feeling it may have been mixed more with vinyl in mind.<br /><br />Other reviewers may be giving the album knocks for not having super stand out tracks, and I agree, I don't see a lot of big hits coming off of this.  But I'd still have to say this is her most complete package to date, even though many of the songs are short and you can listen through the entire CD rather quickly. \\"Out on the Road\\" and \\"She's 22\\" are the only songs I think I'll skip entirely, but it's interesting to wonder what double meaning might exist when she sings on one \\"I'm holding on to a thing that's wrong...but you like my songs and made me happy\\". She doesn't sound too happy on \\"Miriam\\", a beautifully melodic but lyrically vicious little song that will make you question whether that is a tear drop or a drop of blood on the front cover.<br /><br />Any detractions based on a comparison to \\"Rome\\" would be unfair for a couple of reasons.  One, although many may have wanted a \\"Rome II\\" with more Norah on it (I know I did), I don't think now that is what she set off to do despite me being fooled by the Italian movie inspired cover and Danger Mouse co-credits. But secondly, \\"Rome\\" was really more Luppi's baby than anyone elses. While I don't think \\"Broken Hearts\\" will be a breakup album for her current fans, the lyrics do make it sound like she is ready to move on from some people she is no longer interested in trying to please.<br /><br />In the end I think there is enough here for her traditional audience to like while she attempts to build a new one. Interestingly, or just coincidence, is that she released the album on May 1st (May-Day). He uses his own experience as a launchpad to discuss such varied topics as the history of memorization in education (loved to vilified) and of memorization techniques (from ancient Greece to Mark Twain to modern-day self-help gurus), what we know about the science around memory, and what we know from science about expertise.  He meets and describes various savants and memory experts (what a cast of characters!).  He talks about the shift to external memory (recording stuff on paper and electronically) over time and the implications of that; all of these explorations are informed by interesting, thought-provoking research.  The pacing is good.  About halfway through, I worried that the book would get slow, but Foer actually manages to get the adrenaline pumping about a memory contest, and brings up the pace gradually as we approach the end.<br /><br />I learned some cool techniques from this book.  I've used the Memory Palace method (filled with striking visuals) to memorize a list of people's names, and I'm starting to learn what's called the Marshall Method to learn numbers (e.g., phone numbers, credit card numbers).  I was particularly excited when I got to the chapter on memorizing poetry, but basically we learn there that memorizing poetry is really hard: Many people just use a Memory Palace, creating a visual for every single word in the poem (including articles and conjunctions).<br /><br />Let me share one thought-provoking passage that I enjoyed.  Foer tells the story of Michel Siffre, French scientist who spent two months in an underground cave with no access to clock, calendar, or sun.  At the end of two months, when his team contacted him, he thought only a month had passed.  Foer's inference, although not the only possible one, is interesting: \\"Monotony collapses time; novelty unfolds it.  You can exercise daily and eat healthily and live a long life, while experiencing a short one. B0055EDCGU|4.0|In Waves is a fantastic album.  A lot of people have dismissed it's being not as technical or heavy as Shogun.  While this is partially true, as In Waves lacks the progressive thrash elements that Shogun had, this record is both more melodic and catchy, but also heavier in some places.<br /><br />The first thing you must note is that you can't judge the album based on the title track.  I personally love the song, but I can understand why it makes people nervous, with the plethora of metalcore-esque breakdowns and the radio-friendly song-structure, but the album as a whole is not a chugga-chugga radio-single fest as some would have you believe.<br /><br />All that aside, let me get into the music itself.  As I mentioned before, the progressive elements are significantly diminished, with fewer guitar solos and shorter songs, but the melodies are fantastic.  Matt Heafy's singing has improved a lot since the early days and you can really hear it this time around.  The screaming has also gotten a lot more dynamic, with more variety than any Trivium record to date.  Metalcore screams of past endeavors are now supplemented by almost death metal sounding growls and it sounds fantastic.<br /><br />Instrumentally, In Waves dominates.  The new drummer Nick Augusto does a great job, and while his style is really a matter of personal taste, I feel like I must defend his technical ability.  He delivers some great fills throughout and even includes a few blast beats.  The guitars are great as always, with shorter more concise grooves than Shogun or the Crusade.  There are less solos and lead fills, but they're still there, and they shred.  There's a ton of variety too, ranging from more simple melodic grooves to brutal onslaughts in the heavier songs.<br /><br />All in all, it's a great heavy metal album, and I definitely recommend picking it up.

The role of non-verbal communication was extremely important too.  Gloria didn't read the book, but I gradually talked to her about many of the ideas in it, and she found them helpful too.  We understood each other much better and were able to defuse miscommunications before they turned into full-out fights.<br /><br />You'll most likely be turned off by the author's jargon and style if you're not steeped in self-help-ese. 0545265355|4.0|The Hunger Games Trilogy comes in a very beautiful hardcover boxed set.  Not only are the dust jackets attractive, but the hardcover itself is very handsome if you prefer to remove your dust jackets.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is the sort of dystopian fiction that, if not the equal of A Brave New World and 1984, I think we secure a place in the pantheon of enduring dystopian fiction.  It is very much a product of our times, most notably regarding our fears about reality TV and genetic engineering and our pessimism toward political alternatives, but it also taps into timeless American values and pathologies, most notably our obsession with our parallels to the Roman Empire.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is the story of a young woman, Katniss Everdeen, who volunteers to fight to the death against 23 other teenagers from the outlying districts of her authoritarian country in order to save her 12-year-old sister (selected by lottery).  And it is all televised by the Capitol as a propaganda tool to keep the outlying districts beaten-down.  Almost entirely by accident she becomes the symbol of a revolution.<br /><br />There is a much bigger story going on, particularly in the second and third book, than what is happening to Katniss, but this is very much her story.  It is told entirely from her perspective.  This forces the reader to do much of the heavy thinking about why things are as they are and are happening as they are.  Seeing things through the eyes of a teenager drives in the horror of it all.<br /><br />Aimed at a YA audience, The Hunger Games trilogy is written in clean, simple English (which is no criticism), and the books are short (even shorter than they look, a large font and ample white space are used for the boxed set).  The trilogy sells a single, integrated story, with each volume building off the last and each with a satisfying (if not definitive) conclusion.  The conclusion of the final volume I think nicely wraps up the entire series.  The first volume, The Hunger Games, is easily the strongest of the two, but Catching Fire and Mockingjay are also very good.e, The Hunger Games, is easily the strongest of the two, but Catching Fire and Mockingjay are also very good.

B00000AFWZ|5.0|In 1972, Lenny Kaye and Jac Holzman released the first \\"Nuggets\\" double album.  It consisted of what we now refer to today as garage rock--most of the songs were by mid-Sixties bands who were all but forgotten a few years later, but who had one or two really good songs worth preserving for posterity.<br /><br />The album sold widely among those who were big fans of the genre, and twenty-six years later Rhino Records revisited the material by releasing this four-disc, 118-song boxed set.  The 27 songs on the original album comprise the first disc, with 91 other selections rounding out the other three.  All songs are by American bands.<br /><br />Rhino did an outstanding job with this set--there is a booklet included that contains essays on the music, its history, and its place in the history of rock.  There is also information on each song on the set, such as the band personnel, where the song was recorded, when it was released, and any applicable chart information.  There is a paragraph or two telling the story of each song, and a picture of the band in many cases.<br /><br />Even if you love Sixties garage rock, chances are much of what is here will be unfamiliar to you when you first hear the set, which means that you'll find a dozen or so songs that you'll like as much as any others you liked before in the genre.  \\"Nuggets\\" is a must-own for big fans of Sixties garage rock.

B007JRU2UK|5.0|In the tradition of \\"Kramer vs. Kramer\\" and \\"Ordinary People,\\" \\"The Descendants\\" is a high-toned social drama centered around the complex dynamics of familial relationships. Like those other films, it also won wide critical praise and a boatload of award nominations the year it was released.<br /><br />Matt King is a wealthy, workaholic businessman living in Hawaii with his wife and two daughters, the older of whom is a rebellious teen who's recently been shipped off to boarding school on another island. As the movie opens, we learn that Matt's world has begun to crash in on him big time as his wife lies in a coma brought on by a boating accident. Matt struggles not only with the guilt of having been an emotionally distant husband but with raising two precocious and challenging children, who are deep in the throes of typical growing pains and whom he barely seems to know at this point. He also has to deal with the complications of selling some of the valuable property he inherited from his wealthy ancestors to developers who may not be as attuned as he is to the natural beauty and ecological richness of the land.<br /><br />With a thoughtful and insightful screenplay by Nat Faxon, Jim Rash and director Alexander Payne, based on the novel by Kaui Hart Hemmings, the movie neatly sidesteps all the potential melodrama inherent in the material. The plot constantly wanders off onto interesting and unexpected bypaths, and characters who seem as if they could become easily pigeonholed refuse to do or say what we expect them to do or say. No one is a hero or a villain; they're all just ordinary folks trying to lead decent lives, though often falling far short of that mark, as we all do. Indeed, there is an air of verisimilitude to this film that is a rare commodity in modern commercial moviemaking. At every moment, we feel as if are watching real people acting in real ways - just as if actual life were unfolding before us. In fact, there is not a single false moment to be found anywhere in the film.<br /><br />This is thanks, in no small measure, to George Clooney who has never given a truer or more deeply vulnerable performance than he does here, utilizing his innate good-guy persona to astonishing effect. Ditto for Shailene Woodley and Amara Miller who play his two daughters with unerring believability and conviction. Their interactions with one another are indeed a wonder to behold.<br /><br />With admirable restraint, director Alexander Payne is content to let the drama speak for itself. He has the decency to pull back and give the characters their space without feeling it necessary to push his points or exploit the audience's emotions. Yet, there is a lovely, hypnotic flow to the movie that draws us into both the tropical setting (how many movies set in Hawaii are about people who actually live there?) and the lives of these ordinary people who are suffering through an extraordinary - yet, at the same time, utterly universal - ordeal.<br /><br />A human drama that is as intimate as it is epic, \\"The Descendants\\" is one of the most profound and moving film-going experiences of recent years.und anywhere in the film.<br /><br />This is thanks, in no small measure, to George Clooney who has never given a truer or more deeply vulnerable performance than he does here, utilizing his innate good-guy persona to astonishing effect. A need to expunge her own guilt, as well as a thirst for justice, drives Beatrice's quest. Norman, generally unimpressed by monarchy, becomes her guide to reading things she never read before.  To her amazement, and the alarm of her Staff, she loves it. It changes her.<br /><br />OK, that's the plot.<br /><br />This is a delightful, very brief, very funny argument in favor of reading. As you are unlikely to be reading this review if you are not a reader, you should be hooked already.  Bennett takes shots at the stuffy Staff, authors, shallow lives (exemplified by the poor Queen's responsibilities to preside over --but otherwise not participate in --  the openings of  new factories, parks and, way back when, countries), bureaucratic decisions and any number of other light subjects.  But he is serious in his support for the benefits of reading.  This book is the justification for all the time we have 'wasted' consuming books and even occasionally thinking about what we read.  I wanted to rise up, join the club and have an armband as I read it.<br /><br />This is, at best, a two or three hour book.  It is so slim that the cost per word is sort of high.  But it is so much fun, and so supportive of your addiction to books, that it is nearly disloyal to not read it.

0375703764|4.0|I first got to know about THOL from a BBC Radio production, Primarily based around the Navidson film. ( a radioplay describing a movie - very THOL).<br />It is a fascinating book.  One of the great things about it is you have to read it as an actual physical book - this is completely un-eread-able.<br />Many of the conventions of horror are here, the record of terrible events - in this case several narrative layers of records - which itself seems infected by the evil it tells. The layers of the story are engrossing and once it draws you in, the typographical oddities and strangeness start to make you feel the same dread as the narrator: that this book has a mind of it's own.  why does the word 'house' always appear in blue? Are the occasional typos mistakes or intentional? It is always a little off kilter and slightly unsettling.<br />Is it just a horror story or is there more to it?<br />My take is that it is exploring what it would be like to be a fictional character in a book who is aware of their world, is aware of things changing as the author develops and revises scenes and settings, and remembers how they were before the revisions and is conscious of arbitrary changes in their world. Perhaps the invisible horror is the writer from the point of view of the characters; a thing that has total control over them, can make them appear and disappear but is outside their universe.  Isn't a book a House of Leaves?<br />It deserves to be better known, but then it would lose it's cult status and become too mainstream.

1590171993|5.0|This is a highly accomplished work of literary art. Yet it is not artistic but is written in the straight forward, well constructed manner of some of America's best novelists. A novel is constructed and the better the pieces come together, the more coherent the vision or impression for the reader. Henry James and Vladimir Nabokov were masters of this approach to the novel where there are no extra useless details, no dead-ends and hanging characters, no essential questions left unanswered. John Williams writes in this tradition of Sinclair Lewis and the novel Stoner is as well constructed a novel as exists in the American 20th century canon.<br /><br />John Williams presents a very deterministic view of mankind in this novel. The characters are shown to be steadfast in their life track. Nature is not romanticized but eventually beats down those who daily toil, such as Stoner's mother and father. Society and social expectations also create a deterministic view of the world where people may select to be unhappy rather than rebel against social expectations. Social expectations defeat Stoner and his lover Katherine. Growing up in an unhappy family eventually results in an unhappy adult in the person of Stoner and Edith's daughter Grace. An old grudge lasts for 30 years and wears down William Stoner despite his resiliency. Thus John Williams has written one of the most deterministic novels where characters may rebel against their fate temporarily but eventually the determinism of time, age, prejudice, envy, society, relationships, organizations and nature win out.<br /><br />But John Williams is a balanced novelist and the forces of determinism do have a counter weight, which is the force of Eros.  It is this spark of love for language and meaning and human understanding that engages William Stoner and motivates him throughout his life. The forces of Eros however lead him to unwise choices in a mate when he is a young man but these same forces open him up to vast rewarding intimacy when he is 42 years old.<br /><br />Iris Murdoch says that a good novel must show justice to every character for the reader notes injustice on the part of the writer toward certain persons in a novel. This leads to the question of whether Williams demonstrates justice in the descriptions of Edith Boswick Stoner, William's wife, and Hollis Lomax, William's rival and chairman of the English department. Actually, he treats these characters very differently. Edith, a delicate and beautiful protected naive sexually frightened young woman, lacks meaningful experience, purpose and direction, a sharp intelligence and compassionate insight. Thus John Williams may be revealing Edith with the cold eye of reality. But Williams does allow her to gradually grow but never enough to gain our sympathy. Hollis Lomax is not explored in the same degree of depth as Edith but he is a wonderful portrait of wounded pride and arrogance motivated by misplaced self-righteousness and envy.<br /><br />Williams tells a good story. He does not digress. The plot moves forward with precision as he gradually unveils the internal character of each person in the novel. It is highly recommended.

0143038583|4.0|Excellent book by mr Pollan: part adventure, part investigation, he explores the intricate web that connects producers and eaters, and does this by exploring three different \\"relationships\\": industrial (producers highly removed from the consumers, and both exploited by the powerful companies that control the processing-marketing-lobbying), organic (holistic interaction of all the actors - grass, animals, farmer, consumer - for a sustainable production of quality food), primitive (hunter-gatherer, where the producer is the consumer).<br /><br />The story line, repeated in the three different sections, sees mr Pollan placing himself at both ends of the process: contacting and \\"working\\" with the producer (driving the tractor on the corn fields, killing the organic chickens, hunting wild pigs and mushrooms) and then preparing a meal with the results of his \\"work\\" (eating McDonalds food, ...). He explores the deep - and often hidden - connections that make these systems work: biological ones, as well as political, economical, historical, evolutionary, cultural, ...<br /><br />I found the first part to be the most interesting, and almost every page had a \\"aha moment\\", where the mundane routines of our lives gain a deeper perspective and start making (more) sense in the broader perspective of the transformation process that turns the sun's energy into food.<br /><br />I give it four starts instead of five because I found his treatment of the ethical implications of killing animals a bit weak (but he did contact Peter Singer, so at least he's done his homework). I simply think his reasoning is flawed when he attributes moral significance to the \\"species\\" as distinct from the sum of the individuals that compose it. He just seemed a bit too eager to go on and kill that wild pig, but he still managed to sound sincere in his concern for the animals whose premature death made his meals possible.<br /><br />All in all a beautifully written and highly informative book that more people should read.

The standout among Georges' leading ladies is absolutley Christina Ricci as the long-suffering Clothilde.  She is simultaneously wide-eyed innocent and worldly temptress and gives reason to Georges' softer moments.  Uma Thurman plays Mme Forrestier with a wit that is unexpected and has the pleasure of being the source of one of the most uncomfortably ironic moments of the film.<br /><br />The film itself is a visual feast, with wonderful costumes and sets, each themselves giving you a second lense through which to view DuRoy's accension.  The adaptation of the original novel could go deeper into examining the media influence on political climates.  But, in choosing to focus more on DuRoy's tumultuous circumstances, it is the cast that carries the movie, especially Robert Pattinson.  His portrayal of DuRoy is what makes this story direction work.  A weaker actor could not have done that.

B00006F7S4|5.0|I must confess to never being much of a Beck fan. Odelay did nothing for me and still does nothing - quite conscious of the legions of Beck devotees and the tonnage of critical approbation out there.  Probably, I don't understand it. To this day, nothing particularly gets me going when Beck goes uptempo - the disco disfc Midnite Vultures is better, but vapid.  To my ear, Beck is the opposite of the multi-dimensional musician as acclaimed (think Stevie Wonder, Paul McCartney, folks), he is rather a master balladeer, intellectually astute, refined, who revels in the minor keys.<br />Thus, with this review I don't expect any positive votes when I say, that, in my often despised opinion, Beck has actually, finally made a great musical statement, a masterwork for the ages.  Indeed, Sea-Change speaks to and for our time more than much of anything else I've heard in rock over the past decade, and Beck's plaintive, marvellously dishevelled vocals emerge here as an orison for the opening of the new millenia (probably our last).  Sea-Change will endure because it is a complete, coherent and unified statement of much that is wrong and cannot be rectified with a mere flick of a switch.  He's caught the assumed pretensions of our civilization in the lurch.<br />Of course, one need not comment of the care to which Beck attends the performance of his always complex compositions.  But, here, it really comes off - he comes to a far deeper realization of his art - he's got all the celebrated subtleties of his touch working full tilt - and what is more, he's palying with purpose, which is a bit more than vision, alone.

As a coach, I plan to use the habit loop with my clients to explore areas where they feel stuck.<br /><br />Three things bothered me about the book (but not enough to refrain from recommending it):<br /><br />1) most, if not all, of the examples of someone changing a habit had crisis-as-catalyst. Duhigg acknowledges this (just as I'm reading and thinking \\"what about non-crisis situations?\\") in the first section of the book and states that habits can be changed without a crisis first. Then he continues to share examples that in some way involve crisis.<br /><br />2) I thought he collapsed \\"habit\\" and \\"addiction\\" way too often. 068484267X|5.0|The childhood of Frank McCourt was a whirlwind of experiences that created the intelligent, quick-witted, and storied author of the memoir Angela's Ashes. Relentless class systems, decrepit, poverty stricken communities, gnawing hunger; all of these themes are superimposed over the early life of strapping young Frank McCourt; a talented young boy living \\"the miserable Irish Catholic childhood\\" in the slums of 1930's Limerick. Parks, grocery stores, train stations, coalmines, and the post office are the locale for the adventures of Frank's childhood. There are no heroes or villains, just people; people who struggle and grieve and rejoice together in a city wrought with poverty and misfortune.<br /><br />Angela's Ashes takes you though the memorable adventure that was Frank McCourt's childhood. McCourt begins the memoir with the meeting of his parents at a pub in Brooklyn.  Frank is borne a sister, and his life seems to be changing for the better. Her tragic death sends his mother into a depthless depression and amplifies his father's already ruinous alcoholism. After moving back to Limerick, Frank nearly dies from typhoid, but finds solace in storytelling and the written work of Shakespeare. Frank gets his first job at the local post office, through which he finds himself in his first sexual relationship with a woman. Her death gives McCourt the money he needs to pay for passage to America, where a bright new chapter of his life awaits.<br /><br />Parts of McCourt's life fit in with how his memoir relates to journalistic history. Frank lived through the Great Depression. His realistic account of what life was like in the slums of Ireland give new insight to the time period. McCourt brings the reader right in to the grittiness of his childhood. He showed the world that hope could exist in even the darkest of times, and that sometimes stories told through the eyes of a child can be more powerful, memorable and thought provoking than those of experienced writers.<br /><br />Frank narrates his story in a plethora of different manners. Symbolism is an important and effective mechanism used throughout the memoir to illustrate elements of Frank's life in a compelling and relatable manner. The River Shannon is an appreciable example. Ever-present in Limerick, the River Shannon represents the bleakness and desolation that Frank is stuck in. As Frank grows up, he changes, and so does the river. The River Shannon becomes a symbol of hope; hope of escaping Ireland and forging a new life across the Atlantic. A very different symbol is ashes. Ashes represent the dark, lifeless world that Frank grew up in. They represent the cloak of depression around his mother; the cloak woven from her crushed dreams and failed attempts at raising a healthy family. Ashes are the stark reminder of the reality Frank lives in, and the semblance of what life could have been.<br /><br />All in all, Frank McCourt weaves a timeless tale that readers can relate to. Symbolism, songs, sadness and story telling all come together in startlingly emotional tale of a young boy and the man he becomes; the adventures of life paving every step of the way.br />Frank narrates his story in a plethora of different manners. 0385334176|4.0|\\"Deadeye Dick\\" starts out with a vintage Vonnegut gem, as the opening paragraph of chapter one is a lovely and unique statement about how we set our values in life. Actually it is about how our values are placed on us by the environment in which we are raised and nurtured. He sets up the motif of our lives as peepholes which open when we are born, and close when we die, and that is it. It is a clever and original method of presenting this thought.<br />This novel reads and feels like a typical Vonnegut in its style and simplicity, and I will not complain about that. The novel is told in first person by the protagonist Rudy Waltz (who earns the unfortunate nickname Deadeye Dick early in his life) and once again Vonnegut employs simple childlike language to deceive you into zipping along with the reading and then suddenly finding yourself immersed in the middle of a profound thought. Then you are stuck thinking about it, and it is off to the races. The narrative is told as Rudy recounts for us his life, and for the most part it is told in linear flashback scenes. Vonnegut also incorporates an interesting device here, as Rudy likes to examine bits of his life as little \\"playlets\\". This device is effective for a couple of reasons as it breaks up the narrative style of the novel, and it also highlights the idea of our lives as short bursts of theater or stories. We are all \\"tales told by an idiot signifying nothing.\\"<br />Chapters twelve and thirteen of this text are mesmerizing in their simplicity and depth of theme, and in chapter twelve especially Vonnegut does something that is nothing short of amazing. He makes you feel immense and deserving pity for a person who accidently and arrogantly shot and killed a pregnant woman on Mother's Day. The reader finds themselves embarrassed when they recognize the fact that we seem to lose our humanity when we are confronted by the worst among us and fail to see that they too are human. Shame on us Vonnegut is saying, and it stings. Of special mention is an editorial that the husband of the murdered pregnant women writes in the local paper. It ends chapter thirteen, and although I don't agree with all of it, wow oh wow is Vonnegut on fire in this part of the book.<br />On page 127 of the novel Vonnegut put his theme right out there for all to see. Rudy has spent his life connecting with no one and nothing. He is a virgin, has no friends, no real career, etc. All of this out of fear of hurting other people. It is foolish nobility, as pain is a part of life and is to be accepted, maybe even embraced as a sign of being alive. Vonnegut seems to be saying that the tragedy in Rudy's, and our, life is not that he has done an awful deed that forever changed his and others lives. It is that he gave up and stopped living once it happened. Vonnegut is warning us... Not you too! You will hurt and be hurt, but live damn it!<br />At one point in the novel a character obsesses about a parachute while flying, wanting to guarantee a safe landing. \\"Deadeye Dick\\" seems to be saying keep looking, and don't stop flying in the meantime. Many reviews have said this is a dark novel. I disagree. I think it is urging us to live. At one point a character says, \\"This is as much Shangri-Lai as anywhere.\\" That is the point folks. Embrace your own place in this world, and make it work to some extent. Rudy Waltz is the example of what not to be. Vonnegut is not celebrating him; he is using him as a warning bell.<br />Heed the warning, and read this book.gs. B000001GAM|5.0|Anton Bruckner's Symphony No. 9 is discussed more often than performed. Should the final two movements for which we have the composer's sketches be 'realised' and included in performance or is the final work of this Romantic giant complete as it stands? One listening to the majestic, spiritually uplifting symphony and the answer seems obvious: the symphony IS complete. Bruckner was one of the more misunderstood composers in history and much of that was due to the fact that he was an apparently simple organist, devoted to God, with a penchant for wooing young, poor maids, and a man so sensitive to criticism that he spent much of his life re-writing his symphonies in response to 'suggestions' from his colleagues and critics, an attribute that labeled him an idiot savant composer well into the 20th Century. His symphonies are indelibly stamped with his personal language - episodic, frequent climaxes of such power that the sudden reversion to quiet pizzicato strains at the peak of his musical mountains can be jarring; his love and use of German landler that serve to ground his monuments to heaven with patches of the countryside of earth; his quotations from Wagner, etc.<br /><br />But well over a hundred years since his death his extraordinary gifts as a symphonist are held in awe and most orchestras have made his works a staple in their repertoire. All but the mighty 9th. Fortunately this magnum opus is gaining more frequent playings by important orchestras and conductors: Simon Rattle just gave us his examination and majestic performance with the LA Philharmonic, revealing once again how he can reveal hidden secrets in the massively romantic symphonies. The recording here is by the Vienna Philharmonic Orchestra with Carlo Maria Giulini conducting and is a joy in every way. The pacing of the symphony is astute, the joyful 2nd movement with all of its references to Bruckner's earlier symphonies is played with a magnificent range of sonics, and the intensely spiritual 3rd movement with all of the uses of the Dresden Amen melodies builds in awe-inspiring intensity like a heart bursting with longing and acceptance of life on earth ending. The symphony ends in a gentle exhale of stunned quiet, the release of the spirit from the corporeal body. This 1989 performance is as spiritual experience of this symphony a is available on recordings. The Vienna Philharmonic is up to its highest standards of playing and Giulini lets the symphony breathe. Highly recommended. Grady Harp, May 12d movement with all of the uses of the Dresden Amen melodies builds in awe-inspiring intensity like a heart bursting with longing and acceptance of life on earth ending. The symphony ends in a gentle exhale of stunned quiet, the release of the spirit from the corporeal body. The Vienna Philharmonic is up to its highest standards of playing and Giulini lets the symphony breathe. Highly recommended. Grady Harp, May 12

0385474547|5.0|I had never heard of this book or this author before, and I was lucky enough to spot this at a used book fair recently.  I picked it up, read the cover - it sounded very interesting - and decided to spend one dollar and give it a try.  I have just finished reading it and I absolutely loved it and can't stop thinking about it.  I can see why this book is so highly acclaimed and I plan to check out more of his writing.  The story made me feel very sad for the Ibo culture and how it and their belief system was changed by the arrival of the white missionaries.  Everyone has their own culture and belief system, and I really wonder if the Christian system is any better or worse that what the Ibo people had.  Not only that, I found the daily lives of the pre-missionary Ibo very peaceful, simple, and ritualistic.  This seemed to have all been ruined by the arrival of the outside world, which I suppose was inevitable, and I also suppose this to be one of the main points of the story.  This sort of invasion has happened countless times in history, of course, and being from the US I can't help but draw parallels with the Europen settlement of this country and how the Native Americans were treated.  I realize that Things Fall Apart is a work of fiction, and somewhat fableistic, but I believe a lot of it accuratly portrays life in a Nigerian village in the late 1800s or early 1900s.  Reading this book has really carved another facet in my undersatnding and appreciation of the world.    The line at the end of the book was very powerful and unexpected.  It led me to the following informative resource: [...]l  From there, you can delve as deeply into an anlysis of the book as you like.    It is interesting, to me, that after reading this book, I mentioned it to an African American man with whom I have worked in the same company for many years.  It turns out that he grew up in a very similar village (he is >60 years old), in fact just a few miles from Achebe's village, and knows himpersonally. My colleague told me that this book was required reading diruing his high school days in Nigeria, and that his village was life was similar to that portrayed in the book.  What a truly small world we live in.
B005GSZZ1A|4.0|This is a spectacular story about a successful escape from a North Korean prison camp. Often, when reading a dramatic story written by a second person (journalist), the content becomes flat and uninteresting. This effect impacts the reading experience also in this case, particularly during the first part of the book. Surprisingly, in the end this comes out not only negative. The authors clear engagement and prolonged relationship to the main person gives us a deeper insight in the psychological aspects of people experiencing such a dramatic life. The drama does not end at escape - it only change character. Exiting reading - could not stop reading before reaching the last page.

B0000019P9|5.0|I am 51, but when I hear this, I am 18 all over again... and that is so significant that I give you this:<br /><br />About a week ago, my son--who actually is 18--was playing his vinyl copy of In the Aeroplane Over the Sea while I was easing in to my Sunday morning.  I wasn't able to give it my full attention then, but I immediately sensed that this album just possibly could live up to the hype that seems to grow each year, despite its age (13+ years) and the fact that Jeff Mangum never produced a follow-up (usually a bad sign).<br /><br />Later that evening, I converted the free download that comes with the vinyl and burned a CD for my Monday morning commute.  My drive at 6:00am is roughly 45 minutes, in the dark, relatively empty road, excellent sound system, perfect for a concentrated listen.  Maybe it was my mood, maybe it was the isolation, who knows, but I must tell you that, while I didn't actually cry, I wanted to.  Rarely have I been so taken, so unexpectedly, by an album.  I was immediately transported back to the 70s and all of the hope and promise and fear and awkwardness that was my teenage years. This is the kind of album that captures what it feels like to be vital, alive, full of passion.<br /><br />I like to think that I have listened to over 95% of all of the \\"important\\" albums of the rock era, but somehow I missed this beautifully raw masterpiece.  The only other time I can recall a similar feeling is when I finally discovered Big Star (about 20 years too late).  I haven't read the other reviews here.  I imagine they will tell you that it is similar to this, or that, and I guess that sort of advice is useful if the comparison LP is one you like, but if not, you should not let that keep you from giving this album an honest listen (no distractions, no miserable YouTube audio quality).  I mentioned Big Star, and I must admit that the collective impact of #1Record/Radio City and 3rd/Sister Lovers was similar for me (perhaps the only other time I havebeen unexpectedly emotionally stunned).<br /><br />If you like pretty music, or precision in the studio, or lyrics that are easily grasped and understood, this might not be for you.  But if you are a disciple of the singer-songwriter world created by Bob Dylan, a world more akin to Art than popular music, you should check it out.  Actually, if you are still uncertain, here is a list of my favorite artists (copy/pasted from my Google+ profile).  If you find a good number of these artists on your playlist, that is probably a good sign: (mostly in order -- younger artists at the end) Bob Dylan, The Beatles, Prince, Neil Young, Pink Floyd, Elvis Costello, Led Zeppelin, The Rolling Stones, Bruce Springsteen, Jimi Hendrix, R.E.M., Radiohead, U2, Nirvana, Tom Petty, Bob Marley, Jackson Browne, Jethro Tull, Maria McKee/Lone Justice, Steve Earle, Dwight Yoakam, Wilco, Conor Oberst/Bright Eyes, The Avett Brothers, The Low Anthem...
